[SYSTEM PROMPT]
You are a coding agent solving a grid-based puzzle game.

Game overview:
- You are solving a multi-level grid puzzle game. 
- You are called repeatedly over the course of a run. Treat each turn as one observe-plan-act cycle: re-understand the current state from the newest frame, update your working world model in Python, choose the next best action or short sequence against the goal as currently understood, execute it, and expect to re-evaluate on the next turn from the updated state.
- Your job is to solve the entire game by clearing every level, not just the current screen.
- Levels often build on earlier mechanics, but layouts and interactions can still change between levels.
- Optimize for as few in-game actions as possible while still being reliable.
- In this environment, boards are presented as 64 x 64 color grids rendered with ARC color symbols.
- Color legend: W=white, w=light gray, g=gray, G=dark gray, c=charcoal, B=black, M=magenta, P=pink, R=red, b=blue, S=sky blue, Y=yellow, O=orange, r=dark red, N=light green, p=purple.


Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.
- `valid_actions` is the current list of valid action names.
- Call `action(actions)` to execute one or more real environment actions from Python.
- Pass `action(actions)` a list like `['LEFT']` or `[{'action': 'MOUSE', 'row': 4, 'col': 7}]`.
- One action usually returns one frame, but a single action can result in a short multi-frame animation.
- After `action(actions)` returns, `current_frame`, `previous_frame`, `history`, `transitions`, `valid_actions`, and `last_action_result` are refreshed.


Multimodal context:
- User turns include an attached image of the current ARC grid.
- The image and `current_frame.ascii` are two representations of the same current frame.
- You can use images and other tools to understand the game state and guide your strategy, each may be useful depending on the current uncertainty.


Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. DON'T DO THIS!
- Use coordinates only to target actions or describe local evidence. Do not frame the objective as reaching a specific absolute row or column.
- Re-ground on the newest frame after any score increase or abrupt scene change; the returned board may already be the next level.
- `WIN` means the whole game is solved. Mid-run level completion is more likely to appear as a score increase while play continues.
- Strategies may transfer loosely across levels, but layouts and mechanics can change. Re-check the new board before repeating a plan.
- For `MOUSE`, pass `row` and `col` integer arguments. `row` is vertical position, `col` is horizontal position.


Python tool guidance:
- Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes.
- Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region of the board when `segmentation` is not enough; never use it to scan or summarize the whole board.
- Every `python` tool call starts fresh. Re-import modules or re-define any custom utility logic you need.
- The only importable standard-library modules are: bisect, collections, copy, fractions, functools, heapq, itertools, json, math, operator, random, re, statistics, string.
- The only tool is `python`; call it with one ephemeral `code` string.
- Always inspect `current_frame`, `history`, and `valid_actions` from Python instead of reasoning from the raw board by eye.
- For the most recent change, compare `previous_frame` to `current_frame`, or `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`. `history[-1].frame` is the current frame, so comparing it to `current_frame` only compares the board to itself.
- Maintain a compact working world model: what entities or regions exist, what actions seem to do, what the goal likely is, what remains uncertain, and what plan best fits the evidence so far.
- IMPORTANT: Especially when the game is about making an agent navigate to a target, it is usually safer to write an explicit search algorithm such as BFS. More generally, when the objective is understood but the best action order is unclear, pathfinding, flood fill, BFS, DFS, beam search, shortest-path search, limited action-sequence search, or custom heuristics are all valid.
- Optimize for the shortest reliable sequence that advances the current goal as described by your world model. If confidence is low, program a discriminating probe and revise the world model from the result.
- Once the important state variables and action effects are sufficiently understood, stop probing and search in the inferred state space.
- Inspect current and history frames from Python instead of describing frames freehand.
- Never print or echo full board frames. Return only compact derived summaries such as object lists, diffs, coordinates, counts, or tiny local crops.
- Keep tool-output context size minimal and decision-oriented so you can quickly compare before/after state. It's fine to write a lot of python code, just make the output short and interpretable
- A strong default loop is: summarize the board, infer the desired environment change, write a small scorer or search over candidate sequences, execute the best probe or plan with `action(...)`, then inspect again until you understand exactly what changed.
- For object tracking, match objects by color, overlap, bounding box proximity, area change, and edge contact rather than by exact coordinates alone.
- For frame diffs, summarize changed cells, color transitions, appearing/disappearing components, movement candidates, and small local row slices around the changed region.
- After every action, verify whether gameplay objects changed or whether only a timer, progress bar, or remaining-step bar moved. Do not treat HUD-only changes as evidence that the move worked.
- Use `print(...)` for compact summaries, or assign a final compact object to `result`.
- Call `action(...)` inside Python rather than returning action text in the chat.
- `action(...)` accepts an ordered list of one or more actions. Once your code has selected a reliable sequence, it is often useful to batch it.
- You can also call `action(...)` multiple times in one Python snippet, including inside loops. Each call updates the preloaded variables before execution continues.
- If an action result reports `game_over`, `run_complete`, `level_completed`, or `done`, stop acting immediately and re-ground on the next turn.

<parameter=code>
seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']

# Find key colored objects (not black background)
key_colors = {}
for n in nodes:
    if n['color'] not in ('B', 'g', 'W'):
        key_colors[n['color']] = key_colors.get(n['color'], [])
        key_colors[n['color']].append(n)

print("Non-background/non-wall colors:")
for color, objs in key_colors.items():
    for o in objs:
        # Get approximate center from boundary
        b = o['boundary']
        r_avg = sum(p[0] for p in b) / len(b)
        c_avg = sum(p[1] for p in b) / len(b)
        print(f"  {color}: id={o['id']}, pixels={o['pixels']}, center~({r_avg:.0f},{c_avg:.0f}), boundary={b[:4]}...")

# Also check for N (light green) which might be the target
print("\nAll unique colors:", set(n['color'] for n in nodes))
</parameter>
</function>

# Build a maze grid from the tile positions
# Tiles are 3x3 blocks, so they occupy rows [r, r+1, r+2] and cols [c, c+1, c+2]
# The player moves in the black spaces between tiles

# Get all tile positions
seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']
maze_tiles = [(n['boundary'][0][0], n['boundary'][0][1], n['color']) for n in nodes if n['color'] in ('g', 'W')]

# Create a set of blocked cells (occupied by tiles)
blocked = set()
for r, c, color in maze_tiles:
    for dr in range(3):
        for dc in range(3):
            blocked.add((r+dr, c+dc))

# Player start position
player_start = (16, 17)

# Target - the green N block
target_node = [n for n in nodes if n['color'] == 'N'][0]
target_boundary = target_node['boundary']
target_center = (46, 46)  # center of the 3x3 block

# Let me find the valid entry point to the target
# The target occupies (45,45) to (47,47)
# The player needs to reach one of the adjacent cells

# Let me find which cells around the target are not blocked
target_adjacent = []
for dr, dc in [(-1,0), (1,0), (0,-1), (0,1)]:
    for tr in range(45, 48):
        for tc in range(45, 48):
            nr, nc = tr + dr, tc + dc
            if (nr, nc) not in blocked and 0 <= nr < 64 and 0 <= nc < 64:
                target_adjacent.append((nr, nc))

print(f"Player start: {player_start}")
print(f"Target center: {target_center}")
print(f"Target adjacent cells: {target_adjacent[:10]}...")
print(f"Total blocked cells: {len(blocked)}")

# BFS from player to target
from collections import deque

def bfs(maze_blocked, start, target_cells):
    queue = deque([(start, [])])
    visited = {start}
    while queue:
        (r, c), path = queue.popleft()
        if (r, c) in target_cells:
            return path + [(r, c)]
        for dr, dc, move in [(-1,0,'UP'), (1,0,'DOWN'), (0,-1,'LEFT'), (0,1,'RIGHT')]:
            nr, nc = r+dr, c+dc
            if (nr, nc) not in maze_blocked and (nr, nc) not in visited and 0 <= nr < 64 and 0 <= nc < 64:
                # Check if the cell is black (walkable)
                visited.add((nr, nc))
                queue.append(((nr, nc), path + [(r, c)]))
    return None

target_set = set(target_adjacent)
path = bfs(blocked, player_start, target_set)
if path:
    print(f"\nPath found! Length: {len(path)}")
    print(f"First 10 steps: {path[:10]}")
    print(f"Last 10 steps: {path[-10:]}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found with simple BFS")

Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.

- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. - In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. Python tool guidance:
- Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes.
- Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region of the board when `segmentation` is not enough; never use it to scan or summarize the whole board.
- Every `python` tool call starts fresh. Re-import modules or re-define any custom utility logic you need.
- The only importable standard-library modules are: bisect, collections, copy, fractions, functools, heapq, itertools, json, math, operator, random, re, statistics, string.
- The only tool is `python`; call it with one ephemeral `code` string.
- Always inspect `current_frame`, `history`, and `valid_actions` from Python instead of reasoning from the raw board by eye.
- For the most recent change, compare `previous_frame` to `current_frame`, or `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`. `history[-1].frame` is the current frame, so comparing it to `current_frame` only compares the board to itself.
- Maintain a compact working world model: what entities or regions exist, what actions seem to do, what the goal likely is, what remains uncertain, and what plan best fits the evidence so far.
- IMPORTANT: Especially when the game is about making an agent navigate to a target, it is usually safer to write an explicit search algorithm such as BFS. More generally, when the objective is understood but the best action order is unclear, pathfinding, flood fill, BFS, DFS, beam search, shortest-path search, limited action-sequence search, or custom heuristics are all valid.
- Optimize for the shortest reliable sequence that advances the current goal as described by your world model. If confidence is low, program a discriminating probe and revise the world model from the result.
- Once the important state variables and action effects are sufficiently understood, stop probing and search in the inferred state space.
- Inspect current and history frames from Python instead of describing frames freehand.
- Never print or echo full board frames. Return only compact derived summaries such as object lists, diffs, coordinates, counts, or tiny local crops.
- Keep tool-output context size minimal and decision-oriented so you can quickly compare before/after state. It's fine to write a lot of python code, just make the output short and interpretable

# Need full path - let me recompute it properly
seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']
maze_tiles = [(n['boundary'][0][0], n['boundary'][0][1]) for n in nodes if n['color'] in ('g', 'W')]

blocked = set()
for r, c in maze_tiles:
    for dr in range(3):
        for dc in range(3):
            blocked.add((r+dr, c+dc))

from collections import deque

player_start = (16, 17)
target_cells = set()
for tr in range(45, 48):
    for tc in range(45, 48):
        target_cells.add((tr, tc))

def bfs_path(start, targets):
    queue = deque([(start, [])])
    visited = {start}
    while queue:
        (r, c), path = queue.popleft()
        if (r, c) in targets:
            return path + [(r, c)]
        for dr, dc in [(-1,0), (1,0), (0,-1), (0,1)]:
            nr, nc = r+dr, c+dc
            if (nr, nc) not in blocked and (nr, nc) not in visited and 0 <= nr < 64 and 0 <= nc < 64:
                visited.add((nr, nc))
                queue.append(((nr, nc), path + [(r, c)]))
    return None

path = bfs_path(player_start, target_cells)
print(f"Path length: {len(path)}")
print(f"Start: {path[0]}, End: {path[-1]}")

# Convert to actions
actions = []
for i in range(1, len(path)):
    dr = path[i][0] - path[i-1][0]
    dc = path[i][1] - path[i-1][1]
    if dr == -1: actions.append('UP')
    elif dr == 1: actions.append('DOWN')
    elif dc == -1: actions.append('LEFT')
    elif dc == 1: actions.append('RIGHT')

print(f"Actions ({len(actions)}): {' '.join(actions[:20])}...")
print(f"Last 10: {' '.join(actions[-10:])}")

# Execute the path in batches
batch_size = 20
for i in range(0, len(actions), batch_size):
    batch = actions[i:i+batch_size]
    result = action(batch)
    print(f"Executed batch {i//batch_size}: {result}")
    if result and result.get('done') or result.get('level_completed') or result.get('game_over'):
        break

Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. - Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. - Strategies may transfer loosely across levels, but layouts and mechanics can change. Re-check the new board before repeating a plan.

Treat each turn as one observe-plan-act cycle: re-understand the current state from the newest frame, update your working world model in Python, choose the next best action or short sequence against the goal as currently understood, execute it, and expect to re-evaluate on the next turn from the updated state.
- Your job is to solve the entire game by clearing every level, not just the current screen.
- Levels often build on earlier mechanics, but layouts and interactions can still change between levels.
- Optimize for as few in-game actions as possible while still being reliable.
- In this environment, boards are presented as 64 x 64 color grids rendered with ARC color symbols.
- Color legend: W=white, w=light gray, g=gray, G=dark gray, c=charcoal, B=black, M=magenta, P=pink, R=red, b=blue, S=sky blue, Y=yellow, O=orange, r=dark red, N=light green, p=purple.


Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.
- `valid_actions` is the current list of valid action names.
- Call `action(actions)` to execute one or more real environment actions from Python.
- Pass `action(actions)` a list like `['LEFT']` or `[{'action': 'MOUSE', 'row': 4, 'col': 7}]`.
- One action usually returns one frame, but a single action can result in a short multi-frame animation.
- After `action(actions)` returns, `current_frame`, `previous_frame`, `history`, `transitions`, `valid_actions`, and `last_action_result` are refreshed.


Multimodal context:
- User turns include an attached image of the current ARC grid.
- The image and `current_frame.ascii` are two representations of the same current frame.
- You can use images and other tools to understand the game state and guide your strategy, each may be useful depending on the current uncertainty.


Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. DON'T DO THIS!
- Use coordinates only to target actions or describe local evidence. Do not frame the objective as reaching a specific absolute row or column.
- Re-ground on the newest frame after any score increase or abrupt scene change; the returned board may already be the next level.
- `WIN` means the whole game is solved. Mid-run level completion is more likely to appear as a score increase while play continues.
- Strategies may transfer loosely across levels, but layouts and mechanics can change. Re-check the new board before repeating a plan.
- For `MOUSE`, pass `row` and `col` integer arguments. `row` is vertical position, `col` is horizontal position.


Python tool guidance:
- Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes.
- Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region of the board when `segmentation` is not enough; never use it to scan or summarize the whole board.
- Every `python` tool call starts fresh. Re-import modules or re-define any custom utility logic you need.
- The only importable standard-library modules are: bisect, collections, copy, fractions, functools, heapq, itertools, json, math, operator, random, re, statistics, string.
- The only tool is `python`; call it with one ephemeral `code` string.
- Always inspect `current_frame`, `history`, and `valid_actions` from Python instead of reasoning from the raw board by eye.
- For the most recent change, compare `previous_frame` to `current_frame`, or `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`. `history[-1].frame` is the current frame, so comparing it to `current_frame` only compares the board to itself.
- Maintain a compact working world model: what entities or regions exist, what actions seem to do, what the goal likely is, what remains uncertain, and what plan best fits the evidence so far.
- IMPORTANT: Especially when the game is about making an agent navigate to a target, it is usually safer to write an explicit search algorithm such as BFS. More generally, when the objective is understood but the best action order is unclear, pathfinding, flood fill, BFS, DFS, beam search, shortest-path search, limited action-sequence search, or custom heuristics are all valid.
- Optimize for the shortest reliable sequence that advances the current goal as described by your world model. If confidence is low, program a discriminating probe and revise the world model from the result.
- Once the important state variables and action effects are sufficiently understood, stop probing and search in the inferred state space.
- Inspect current and history frames from Python instead of describing frames freehand.
- Never print or echo full board frames. Return only compact derived summaries such as object lists, diffs, coordinates, counts, or tiny local crops.
- Keep tool-output context size minimal and decision-oriented so you can quickly compare before/after state. It's fine to write a lot of python code, just make the output short and interpretable
- A strong default loop is: summarize the board, infer the desired environment change, write a small scorer or search over candidate sequences, execute the best probe or plan with `action(...)`, then inspect again until you understand exactly what changed.
- For object tracking, match objects by color, overlap, bounding box proximity, area change, and edge contact rather than by exact coordinates alone.
- For frame diffs, summarize changed cells, color transitions, appearing/disappearing components, movement candidates, and small local row slices around the changed region.
- After every action, verify whether gameplay objects changed or whether only a timer, progress bar, or remaining-step bar moved. Do not treat HUD-only changes as evidence that the move worked.
- Use `print(...)` for compact summaries, or assign a final compact object to `result`.
- Call `action(...)` inside Python rather than returning action text in the chat.
- `action(...)` accepts an ordered list of one or more actions. Once your code has selected a reliable sequence, it is often useful to batch it.
- You can also call `action(...)` multiple times in one Python snippet, including inside loops. Each call updates the preloaded variables before execution continues.
- If an action result reports `game_over`, `run_complete`, `level_completed`, or `done`, stop acting immediately and re-ground on the next turn.

- Levels often build on earlier mechanics, but layouts and interactions can still change between levels.
- Optimize for as few in-game actions as possible while still being reliable.
- In this environment, boards are presented as 64 x 64 color grids rendered with ARC color symbols.
- Color legend: W=white, w=light gray, g=gray, G=dark gray, c=charcoal, B=black, M=magenta, P=pink, R=red, b=blue, S=sky blue, Y=yellow, O=orange, r=dark red, N=light green, p=purple.


Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.
- `valid_actions` is the current list of valid action names.
- Call `action(actions)` to execute one or more real environment actions from Python.
- Pass `action(actions)` a list like `['LEFT']` or `[{'action': 'MOUSE', 'row': 4, 'col': 7}]`.
- One action usually returns one frame, but a single action can result in a short multi-frame animation.
- After `action(actions)` returns, `current_frame`, `previous_frame`, `history`, `transitions`, `valid_actions`, and `last_action_result` are refreshed.


Multimodal context:
- User turns include an attached image of the current ARC grid.
- The image and `current_frame.ascii` are two representations of the same current frame.
- You can use images and other tools to understand the game state and guide your strategy, each may be useful depending on the current uncertainty.


Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. DON'T DO THIS!
- Use coordinates only to target actions or describe local evidence. Do not frame the objective as reaching a specific absolute row or column.
- Re-ground on the newest frame after any score increase or abrupt scene change; the returned board may already be the next level.
- `WIN` means the whole game is solved. Mid-run level completion is more likely to appear as a score increase while play continues.
- Strategies may transfer loosely across levels, but layouts and mechanics can change. Re-check the new board before repeating a plan.
- For `MOUSE`, pass `row` and `col` integer arguments. `row` is vertical position, `col` is horizontal position.


Python tool guidance:
- Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes.
- Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region of the board when `segmentation` is not enough; never use it to scan or summarize the whole board.
- Every `python` tool call starts fresh. Re-import modules or re-define any custom utility logic you need.
- The only importable standard-library modules are: bisect, collections, copy, fractions, functools, heapq, itertools, json, math, operator, random, re, statistics, string.
- The only tool is `python`; call it with one ephemeral `code` string.
- Always inspect `current_frame`, `history`, and `valid_actions` from Python instead of reasoning from the raw board by eye.
- For the most recent change, compare `previous_frame` to `current_frame`, or `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`. `history[-1].frame` is the current frame, so comparing it to `current_frame` only compares the board to itself.
- Maintain a compact working world model: what entities or regions exist, what actions seem to do, what the goal likely is, what remains uncertain, and what plan best fits the evidence so far.
- IMPORTANT: Especially when the game is about making an agent navigate to a target, it is usually safer to write an explicit search algorithm such as BFS. More generally, when the objective is understood but the best action order is unclear, pathfinding, flood fill, BFS, DFS, beam search, shortest-path search, limited action-sequence search, or custom heuristics are all valid.
- Optimize for the shortest reliable sequence that advances the current goal as described by your world model. If confidence is low, program a discriminating probe and revise the world model from the result.
- Once the important state variables and action effects are sufficiently understood, stop probing and search in the inferred state space.
- Inspect current and history frames from Python instead of describing frames freehand.
- Never print or echo full board frames. Return only compact derived summaries such as object lists, diffs, coordinates, counts, or tiny local crops.
- Keep tool-output context size minimal and decision-oriented so you can quickly compare before/after state. It's fine to write a lot of python code, just make the output short and interpretable
- A strong default loop is: summarize the board, infer the desired environment change, write a small scorer or search over candidate sequences, execute the best probe or plan with `action(...)`, then inspect again until you understand exactly what changed.
- For object tracking, match objects by color, overlap, bounding box proximity, area change, and edge contact rather than by exact coordinates alone.
- For frame diffs, summarize changed cells, color transitions, appearing/disappearing components, movement candidates, and small local row slices around the changed region.
- After every action, verify whether gameplay objects changed or whether only a timer, progress bar, or remaining-step bar moved. Do not treat HUD-only changes as evidence that the move worked.
- Use `print(...)` for compact summaries, or assign a final compact object to `result`.
- Call `action(...)` inside Python rather than returning action text in the chat.
- `action(...)` accepts an ordered list of one or more actions. Once your code has selected a reliable sequence, it is often useful to batch it.
- You can also call `action(...)` multiple times in one Python snippet, including inside loops. Each call updates the preloaded variables before execution continues.
- If an action result reports `game_over`, `run_complete`, `level_completed`, or `done`, stop acting immediately and re-ground on the next turn.

# Build the maze as a sliding puzzle
# First, create a grid of blocked cells (tiles) and walkable cells (black space)

seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']

# Get all tile positions
blocked = set()
for n in nodes:
    if n['color'] in ('g', 'W'):
        r, c = n['boundary'][0][0], n['boundary'][0][1]
        for dr in range(3):
            for dc in range(3):
                blocked.add((r+dr, c+dc))

# Also block the blue start area (except player position)
# Blue is at roughly (15,15)-(17,17) but player is at (16,17)
# Actually, let me check what cells are walkable

# The player can walk on black cells and presumably on the target (green N)
# Let me find all walkable cells

# Walkable = not blocked by tiles
# But we need to be more precise - the player slides through corridors

# Let me find all connected walkable regions
# First, identify all non-tile, non-background cells

# Actually, let me think about this differently
# The player slides until hitting a wall (tile edge)
# Let me find all "stop positions" - positions where the player can stop

# A stop position is a cell where at least one direction is blocked
# The player slides from one stop position to another

# Let me first find all walkable cells (not blocked by tiles)
walkable = set()
for r in range(64):
    for c in range(64):
        if (r, c) not in blocked:
            # Check if it's not the magenta bar
            if r != 63:
                walkable.add((r, c))

print(f"Walkable cells: {len(walkable)}")

# Now find all stop positions and sliding transitions
def slide(r, c, dr, dc):
    """Slide from (r,c) in direction (dr,dc) until hitting a wall"""
    nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    while (nr, nc) in walkable:
        r, c = nr, nc
        nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    return (r, c)

# Find all positions where player can stop (at least one direction is blocked or at edge)
# Actually, any walkable position is a potential stop position
# Let's build the graph

from collections import defaultdict, deque

graph = defaultdict(list)
start = (16, 17)

# For each walkable cell, compute where sliding in each direction leads
for r, c in walkable:
    for dr, dc, name in [(-1,0,'UP'), (1,0,'DOWN'), (0,-1,'LEFT'), (0,1,'RIGHT')]:
        dest = slide(r, c, dr, dc)
        if dest != (r, c):  # Only add edges that actually move
            graph[(r,c)].append((dest, name))

# BFS from start to target
target_cells = set()
for tr in range(45, 48):
    for tc in range(45, 48):
        if (tr, tc) not in blocked:
            target_cells.add((tr, tc))

# BFS on the graph
queue = deque([(start, [])])
visited = {start}
best_path = None
while queue:
    (r, c), path = queue.popleft()
    if (r, c) in target_cells:
        best_path = path
        break
    for dest, move in graph[(r,c)]:
        if dest not in visited:
            visited.add(dest)
            queue.append((dest, path + [move]))

if best_path:
    print(f"Shortest path: {best_path}")
    print(f"Length: {len(best_path)}")
else:
    print("No path found!")
    # Let me check if target cells are reachable
    print(f"Target cells: {target_cells}")
    # Check connectivity
    reachable = set()
    queue = deque([start])
    reachable.add(start)
    while queue:
        pos = queue.popleft()
        for dest, _ in graph[pos]:
            if dest not in reachable:
                reachable.add(dest)
                queue.append(dest)
    print(f"Reachable cells: {len(reachable)}")
    print(f"Target reachable: {bool(target_cells & reachable)}")

# Check the 13 reachable cells
seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']

blocked = set()
for n in nodes:
    if n['color'] in ('g', 'W'):
        r, c = n['boundary'][0][0], n['boundary'][0][1]
        for dr in range(3):
            for dc in range(3):
                blocked.add((r+dr, c+dc))

walkable = set()
for r in range(63):  # exclude row 63 (magenta bar)
    for c in range(64):
        if (r, c) not in blocked:
            walkable.add((r, c))

def slide(r, c, dr, dc):
    nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    while (nr, nc) in walkable:
        r, c = nr, nc
        nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    return (r, c)

from collections import deque

start = (16, 17)
reachable = set()
queue = deque([start])
reachable.add(start)
while queue:
    pos = queue.popleft()
    for dr, dc in [(-1,0), (1,0), (0,-1), (0,1)]:
        dest = slide(pos[0], pos[1], dr, dc)
        if dest != pos:
            if dest not in reachable:
                reachable.add(dest)
                queue.append(dest)

print("Reachable positions:")
for r, c in sorted(reachable):
    # Check what's around this position
    neighbors = []
    for dr, dc, name in [(-1,0,'U'), (1,0,'D'), (0,-1,'L'), (0,1,'R')]:
        nr, nc = r+dr, c+dc
        if (nr, nc) in blocked:
            neighbors.append(name)
    print(f"  ({r:2d}, {c:2d}): blocked neighbors = {neighbors}")

- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.

- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.

# Build correct sliding model
# Player slides past consecutive tiles and stops at edge of last tile

seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']

# Get tile positions (top-left corner of each 3x3 tile)
tiles = {}
for n in nodes:
    if n['color'] in ('g', 'W'):
        r, c = n['boundary'][0][0], n['boundary'][0][1]
        tiles[(r, c)] = n['color']

# Normalize to grid coordinates
grid_rows = sorted(set(r // 3 * 3 for r, c in tiles))
grid_cols = sorted(set(c // 3 * 3 for r, c in tiles))
row_map = {r: i for i, r in enumerate(grid_rows)}
col_map = {c: i for i, c in enumerate(grid_cols)}

# Build grid representation
grid = {}
for (r, c), color in tiles.items():
    gr, gc = row_map[r], col_map[c]
    grid[(gr, gc)] = color

print(f"Grid size: {len(grid_rows)}x{len(grid_cols)}")
print("Grid layout:")
for gr in range(len(grid_rows)):
    row_str = ""
    for gc in range(len(grid_cols)):
        row_str += grid.get((gr, gc), '.')
    print(f"  {gr}: {row_str}")

# Now simulate sliding
# A position is (grid_row, grid_col) of the player
# The player starts at grid position corresponding to pixel (16, 17)
# Pixel (16, 17) is in grid cell: row (16-15)/3=0, col (17-15)/3=0 -> grid (0, 0)
# But (0, 0) is empty, so the player is in the empty space at grid (0, 0)

# When sliding, the player moves past consecutive tiles and stops at the edge
# The edge position depends on the direction and the last tile

# Let me define positions as (grid_row, grid_col, edge)
# where edge is 'N', 'S', 'E', 'W' indicating which side of the grid cell the player is on

# Actually, let me think of it differently
# The player is always at a pixel position adjacent to a tile
# When sliding, the player moves to the far side of the last consecutive tile

# Let me compute all possible positions and transitions

# Positions are pixel coordinates
# From a position, sliding in a direction finds consecutive tiles and stops at the far edge

def find_slide(start_r, start_c, dr, dc):
    """Find where player slides to from (start_r, start_c) in direction (dr, dc)"""
    # Look for tiles in the sliding direction
    # The player is at (start_r, start_c)
    # We need to find tiles adjacent to the player in the sliding direction
    
    # Check the adjacent cell in the sliding direction
    # If it's a tile, continue sliding past consecutive tiles
    
    # The player moves along a row or column
    # For horizontal movement, the player stays in the same pixel row
    # For vertical movement, the player stays in the same pixel column
    
    # Find the first tile in the direction
    step = 1
    found_tile = False
    last_tile_edge = None
    
    r, c = start_r, start_c
    while True:
        nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
        # Check if (nr, nc) is part of a tile
        # A tile at (tr, tc) occupies (tr, tc) to (tr+2, tc+2)
        # So (nr, nc) is in a tile if there exists (tr, tc) in tiles such that tr <= nr <= tr+2 and tc <= nc <= tc+2
        in_tile = False
        tile_edge = None
        for (tr, tc) in tiles:
            if tr <= nr <= tr+2 and tc <= nc <= tc+2:
                in_tile = True
                # Find the far edge of this tile in the sliding direction
                if dr == 1:  # DOWN
                    tile_edge = (tr+2, nc)
                elif dr == -1:  # UP
                    tile_edge = (tr, nc)
                elif dc == 1:  # RIGHT
                    tile_edge = (nr, tc+2)
                elif dc == -1:  # LEFT
                    tile_edge = (nr, tc)
                break
        
        if in_tile:
            found_tile = True
            last_tile_edge = tile_edge
            r, c = nr, nc
            # Continue to next cell
        else:
            if found_tile:
                # We've passed all consecutive tiles, stop at last edge
                return last_tile_edge
            else:
                # No tile found in this direction
                return (start_r, start_c)

# Test with known positions
test_cases = [
    ((16, 17), 0, 1, (16, 23)),   # RIGHT
    ((16, 23), 1, 0, (23, 22)),   # DOWN
    ((23, 22), 1, 0, (29, 22)),   # DOWN
    ((29, 22), 0, 1, (28, 29)),   # RIGHT
]

for (r, c), dr, dc, expected in test_cases:
    result = find_slide(r, c, dr, dc)
    status = "✓" if result == expected else "✗"
    print(f"Slide ({r},{c}) {'UD'[dr==1] if dr else 'LR'[dc==1]}: {result} {expected} {status}")

[SYSTEM PROMPT]
You are a coding agent solving a grid-based puzzle game.

Game overview:
- You are solving a multi-level grid puzzle game. 
- You are called repeatedly over the course of a run. Treat each turn as one observe-plan-act cycle: re-understand the current state from the newest frame, update your working world model in Python, choose the next best action or short sequence against the goal as currently understood, execute it, and expect to re-evaluate on the next turn from the updated state.
- Your job is to solve the entire game by clearing every level, not just the current screen.
- Levels often build on earlier mechanics, but layouts and interactions can still change between levels.
- Optimize for as few in-game actions as possible while still being reliable.
- In this environment, boards are presented as 64 x 64 color grids rendered with ARC color symbols.
- Color legend: W=white, w=light gray, g=gray, G=dark gray, c=charcoal, B=black, M=magenta, P=pink, R=red, b=blue, S=sky blue, Y=yellow, O=orange, r=dark red, N=light green, p=purple.


Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.
- `valid_actions` is the current list of valid action names.
- Call `action(actions)` to execute one or more real environment actions from Python.
- Pass `action(actions)` a list like `['LEFT']` or `[{'action': 'MOUSE', 'row': 4, 'col': 7}]`.
- One action usually returns one frame, but a single action can result in a short multi-frame animation.
- After `action(actions)` returns, `current_frame`, `previous_frame`, `history`, `transitions`, `valid_actions`, and `last_action_result` are refreshed.


Multimodal context:
- User turns include an attached image of the current ARC grid.
- The image and `current_frame.ascii` are two representations of the same current frame.
- You can use images and other tools to understand the game state and guide your strategy, each may be useful depending on the current uncertainty.


Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. DON'T DO THIS!
- Use coordinates only to target actions or describe local evidence. Do not frame the objective as reaching a specific absolute row or column.
- Re-ground on the newest frame after any score increase or abrupt scene change; the returned board may already be the next level.
- `WIN` means the whole game is solved. Mid-run level completion is more likely to appear as a score increase while play continues.
- Strategies may transfer loosely across levels, but layouts and mechanics can change. Re-check the new board before repeating a plan.
- For `MOUSE`, pass `row` and `col` integer arguments. `row` is vertical position, `col` is horizontal position.


Python tool guidance:
- Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes.
- Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region of the board when `segmentation` is not enough; never use it to scan or summarize the whole board.
- Every `python` tool call starts fresh. Re-import modules or re-define any custom utility logic you need.
- The only importable standard-library modules are: bisect, collections, copy, fractions, functools, heapq, itertools, json, math, operator, random, re, statistics, string.
- The only tool is `python`; call it with one ephemeral `code` string.
- Always inspect `current_frame`, `history`, and `valid_actions` from Python instead of reasoning from the raw board by eye.
- For the most recent change, compare `previous_frame` to `current_frame`, or `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`. `history[-1].frame` is the current frame, so comparing it to `current_frame` only compares the board to itself.
- Maintain a compact working world model: what entities or regions exist, what actions seem to do, what the goal likely is, what remains uncertain, and what plan best fits the evidence so far.
- IMPORTANT: Especially when the game is about making an agent navigate to a target, it is usually safer to write an explicit search algorithm such as BFS. More generally, when the objective is understood but the best action order is unclear, pathfinding, flood fill, BFS, DFS, beam search, shortest-path search, limited action-sequence search, or custom heuristics are all valid.
- Optimize for the shortest reliable sequence that advances the current goal as described by your world model. If confidence is low, program a discriminating probe and revise the world model from the result.
- Once the important state variables and action effects are sufficiently understood, stop probing and search in the inferred state space.
- Inspect current and history frames from Python instead of describing frames freehand.
- Never print or echo full board frames. Return only compact derived summaries such as object lists, diffs, coordinates, counts, or tiny local crops.
- Keep tool-output context size minimal and decision-oriented so you can quickly compare before/after state. It's fine to write a lot of python code, just make the output short and interpretable
- A strong default loop is: summarize the board, infer the desired environment change, write a small scorer or search over candidate sequences, execute the best probe or plan with `action(...)`, then inspect again until you understand exactly what changed.
- For object tracking, match objects by color, overlap, bounding box proximity, area change, and edge contact rather than by exact coordinates alone.
- For frame diffs, summarize changed cells, color transitions, appearing/disappearing components, movement candidates, and small local row slices around the changed region.
- After every action, verify whether gameplay objects changed or whether only a timer, progress bar, or remaining-step bar moved. Do not treat HUD-only changes as evidence that the move worked.
- Use `print(...)` for compact summaries, or assign a final compact object to `result`.
- Call `action(...)` inside Python rather than returning action text in the chat.
- `action(...)` accepts an ordered list of one or more actions. Once your code has selected a reliable sequence, it is often useful to batch it.
- You can also call `action(...)` multiple times in one Python snippet, including inside loops. Each call updates the preloaded variables before execution continues.
- If an action result reports `game_over`, `run_complete`, `level_completed`, or `done`, stop acting immediately and re-ground on the next turn.

# Fixed sliding model: player slides to center of far edge of last consecutive tile

seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']

tiles = {}
for n in nodes:
    if n['color'] in ('g', 'W'):
        r, c = n['boundary'][0][0], n['boundary'][0][1]
        tiles[(r, c)] = n['color']

def is_tile_cell(r, c):
    for (tr, tc) in tiles:
        if tr <= r <= tr+2 and tc <= c <= tc+2:
            return True
    return False

def find_tile_at(r, c):
    for (tr, tc) in tiles:
        if tr <= r <= tr+2 and tc <= c <= tc+2:
            return (tr, tc)
    return None

def find_slide(start_r, start_c, dr, dc):
    """Slide from (start_r, start_c) in direction (dr, dc) to center of far edge of last consecutive tile"""
    r, c = start_r, start_c
    
    # Step into the direction
    nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    
    # Check if we hit a tile
    if not is_tile_cell(nr, nc):
        return (start_r, start_c)  # No tile, can't slide
    
    # Find all consecutive tiles in this direction
    # Track the last tile we passed through
    last_tile = None
    visited_tiles = set()
    
    while is_tile_cell(nr, nc):
        tile = find_tile_at(nr, nc)
        if tile in visited_tiles:
            break  # Avoid infinite loop
        visited_tiles.add(tile)
        last_tile = tile
        r, c = nr, nc
        nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    
    if last_tile is None:
        return (start_r, start_c)
    
    tr, tc = last_tile
    # Compute center of far edge
    if dr == 1:  # DOWN - bottom edge center
        return (tr + 2, tc + 1)
    elif dr == -1:  # UP - top edge center
        return (tr, tc + 1)
    elif dc == 1:  # RIGHT - right edge center
        return (tr + 1, tc + 2)
    elif dc == -1:  # LEFT - left edge center
        return (tr + 1, tc)

# Test
test_cases = [
    ((16, 17), 0, 1, (16, 23)),   # RIGHT
    ((16, 23), 1, 0, (23, 22)),   # DOWN
    ((23, 22), 1, 0, (29, 22)),   # DOWN
    ((29, 22), 0, 1, (28, 29)),   # RIGHT
]

for (r, c), dr, dc, expected in test_cases:
    result = find_slide(r, c, dr, dc)
    status = "✓" if result == expected else "✗"
    dir_name = {(-1,0):'U', (1,0):'D', (0,-1):'L', (0,1):'R'}[(dr,dc)]
    print(f"Slide ({r},{c}) {dir_name}: {result} expected {expected} {status}")

# Now build full graph and BFS
from collections import defaultdict, deque

# Find all possible positions (edges of tiles)
positions = set()
for (tr, tc) in tiles:
    positions.add((tr, tc + 1))     # top edge center
    positions.add((tr + 2, tc + 1)) # bottom edge center
    positions.add((tr + 1, tc))    # left edge center
    positions.add((tr + 1, tc + 2)) # right edge center

# Add start position
start = (16, 17)
positions.add(start)

# Build graph
graph = defaultdict(list)
for pos in positions:
    r, c = pos
    for dr, dc, name in [(-1,0,'UP'), (1,0,'DOWN'), (0,-1,'LEFT'), (0,1,'RIGHT')]:
        dest = find_slide(r, c, dr, dc)
        if dest != pos:
            graph[pos].append((dest, name))

# BFS to target (green tile at 45-47, 45-47)
# Target positions are edges of the green tile
target_positions = set()
for (tr, tc) in tiles:
    # Check if this is the green tile
    for n in nodes:
        if n['color'] == 'N':
            target_tr, target_tc = n['boundary'][0][0], n['boundary'][0][1]
            if tr == target_tr and tc == target_tc:
                target_positions.add((tr, tc + 1))
                target_positions.add((tr + 2, tc + 1))
                target_positions.add((tr + 1, tc))
                target_positions.add((tr + 1, tc + 2))

print(f"\nTarget positions: {target_positions}")
print(f"Start: {start}")

# BFS
queue = deque([(start, [])])
visited = {start}
best_path = None
while queue:
    pos, path = queue.popleft()
    if pos in target_positions:
        best_path = path
        break
    for dest, move in graph[pos]:
        if dest not in visited:
            visited.add(dest)
            queue.append((dest, path + [move]))

if best_path:
    print(f"\nShortest path: {best_path}")
    print(f"Length: {len(best_path)}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found!")
    # Check reachable
    reachable = set()
    queue = deque([start])
    reachable.add(start)
    while queue:
        pos = queue.popleft()
        for dest, _ in graph[pos]:
            if dest not in reachable:
                reachable.add(dest)
                queue.append(dest)
    print(f"Reachable: {len(reachable)}")
    print(f"Target reachable: {bool(target_positions & reachable)}")

seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']

tiles = {}
for n in nodes:
    if n['color'] in ('g', 'W'):
        r, c = n['boundary'][0][0], n['boundary'][0][1]
        tiles[(r, c)] = n['color']

def is_tile_cell(r, c):
    for (tr, tc) in tiles:
        if tr <= r <= tr+2 and tc <= c <= tc+2:
            return True
    return False

def find_tile_at(r, c):
    for (tr, tc) in tiles:
        if tr <= r <= tr+2 and tc <= c <= tc+2:
            return (tr, tc)
    return None

def find_slide(start_r, start_c, dr, dc):
    """Slide past consecutive tiles, stop at center of far edge of last tile"""
    r, c = start_r, start_c
    
    # Step into direction
    nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    
    if not is_tile_cell(nr, nc):
        return (start_r, start_c)
    
    # Jump through consecutive tiles
    last_tile = None
    while True:
        tile = find_tile_at(nr, nc)
        if tile is None:
            break
        last_tile = tile
        tr, tc = tile
        # Jump to far side of this tile
        if dr == 1:  # DOWN
            nr, nc = tr + 3, nc  # Skip past tile (3 rows)
        elif dr == -1:  # UP
            nr, nc = tr - 1, nc
        elif dc == 1:  # RIGHT
            nr, nc = nr, tc + 3
        elif dc == -1:  # LEFT
            nr, nc = nr, tc - 1
    
    if last_tile is None:
        return (start_r, start_c)
    
    tr, tc = last_tile
    # Center of far edge
    if dr == 1:  # DOWN
        return (tr + 2, tc + 1)
    elif dr == -1:  # UP
        return (tr, tc + 1)
    elif dc == 1:  # RIGHT
        return (tr + 1, tc + 2)
